  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ced22c18-5b4f-4557-9fa6-7ef31291c091'

<span id="freeTextContainer6609060395604883080">Bailed out of jail and holed up in a low-rent motel, amnesiac Eric Ashworth's only memory is a woman's name: Desiree. With steadily increasing doses of a strange new hallucinogen, Eric finds that the drug allows him to reassemble his past in broken fragments. But as he begins to lose touch with the present, his distinction between truth and fantasy begins to crumble, creat</span>
  <span id="freeText6609060395604883080" style="display:none">Bailed out of jail and holed up in a low-rent motel, amnesiac Eric Ashworth's only memory is a woman's name: Desiree. With steadily increasing doses of a strange new hallucinogen, Eric finds that the drug allows him to reassemble his past in broken fragments. But as he begins to lose touch with the present, his distinction between truth and fantasy begins to crumble, creating a world where divisions between love and loss, violence and tenderness, and fact and fiction are less discernible than they ought to be.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7767660146319401423">I love this man's writing. The words so poetic and the story unfolds like a bad dream, and you get the workings of a man who suffers from amnesia and is starting to get his memory back in chunks, but you can't be certain if any of those flashes are true. Very abstract, like Memento meets Breaking Bad. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_274162513" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/274162513" />
    <a title="Daniel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7664145-daniel"><img alt="Daniel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594511303p2/7664145.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/274162513?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel" href="/user/show/7664145-daniel">Daniel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer274162513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16996541735286804965">I was going to start this review by comparing Clevenger's writing to that of Chuck Palahniuk and Will Christopher Baer. He's got the pace and acerbic plot-mind of one and the visceral, dizzying prose of the other. Then I flipped to the acknowledgements, and there, on the second paragraph, Clevenger thanks them both. "Well, no wonder," I thought. Fans of either (or both) Palahniuk and Baer are bound to love "Dermaphoria."<br/><br/>Clevenger starts with a classic (and almost trite) premise: a man wakes up i</span>
  <span id="freeText16996541735286804965" style="display:none">I was going to start this review by comparing Clevenger's writing to that of Chuck Palahniuk and Will Christopher Baer. He's got the pace and acerbic plot-mind of one and the visceral, dizzying prose of the other. Then I flipped to the acknowledgements, and there, on the second paragraph, Clevenger thanks them both. "Well, no wonder," I thought. Fans of either (or both) Palahniuk and Baer are bound to love "Dermaphoria."<br /><br />Clevenger starts with a classic (and almost trite) premise: a man wakes up in a hospital, and the only thing he knows is one name -- Desiree. The man (Eric Ashworth) -- sought after by info-hungry cops, antsy attorneys, and dispassionate mobsters -- attempts to piece together his life, bit by bit, around that one name, using both old-fashioned persistence and a new street drug. The substance, called Skin, synthesizes the sense of touch, using it to extract reality-rich remembrances from an ever-expanding history ("Having more memory is just a way of distorting a greater amount of the past," says one character). The only problem? Skin comes with some pretty serious side effects. And, furthermore, who's to say that the results are 100% accurate?<br /><br />One part mystery, one part noir, and every bit of it a puzzle of firey, arresting prose, "Dermaphoria" is a great book. Ashworth's disorientation is made all the more palpable by Clevenger's crackling writing. Some might find the descriptions over-written, but I'd say he hasn't written enough. This 214 page novel is a quick read, ending faster than it takes a firefly to blink. Clevenger's descriptions are hefty and mobile, apt and stunning, and everything is slathered with import (even the names themselves, Ashworth and Desiree and others, are totems for a larger point). This book is about more than just drug overdoses and regrets, and like his contemporaries (even more so than Palahniuk, I'd say), Clevenger refuses to dilute his tale with bromides or easy outs.<br /><br />However, even if you don't have the inclination to dig beneath the topsoil of Clevenger's mesmerizing world, you can still enjoy the lusciously dirty surface. It's a tale that is rewarding on multiple levels, superficial or subliminal, and although the ending is chaotic and heart-breaking (Clevenger's male protagonists never seem to catch a break), it also proves there's a real heart there to be broken. Dark, smart, gritty, and spare, "Dermaphoria" gets under the skin and stays there. You should read it, and then read it again.<br /><br />In case you forgot anything.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16996541735286804965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16976081538356287143">Do you like Breaking Bad?<br/>If yes, then read this book, as it is more or less a whole season of breaking bad that was never filmed. Perhaps intentionally perhaps unintentionally. Despite it being a clear parallel there are certain paragraphs in this book that make you smarter for reading them, they make you more interesting, they are instantly copy and highlighted and you hope to say something similar at some point in your life.<br/><br/>There are moments in this okay story where the writing is so pure it m</span>
  <span id="freeText16976081538356287143" style="display:none">Do you like Breaking Bad?<br />If yes, then read this book, as it is more or less a whole season of breaking bad that was never filmed. Perhaps intentionally perhaps unintentionally. Despite it being a clear parallel there are certain paragraphs in this book that make you smarter for reading them, they make you more interesting, they are instantly copy and highlighted and you hope to say something similar at some point in your life.<br /><br />There are moments in this okay story where the writing is so pure it makes me believe he held onto the thought for years and built and entire story around it. And I can't help but sit back and think. <br />God. <br />Damn.<br />You.<br /><br />Because they are perfect, and they are the way lines are supposed to be delivered. And I'm jealous and I'm a better writer for having read those paragraphs from this book.<br /><br />The story itself is good, but if I had to read another 200 pages just for those little gold nuggets, for me to pick them up, dust them off and then show them shining and new to the uninitiated, I would read those 200 pages with the same anticipation I have for water slides and cheesecake. </span>

<div id="review_659590906" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="L.V. Sage" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10507215-l-v-sage"><img alt="L.V. Sage" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404403593p2/10507215.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/659590906?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="L.V. Sage" class="user" itemprop="url" name="L.V. Sage" href="/user/show/10507215-l-v-sage">L.V. Sage</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10507215-l-v-sage?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer659590906" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12079679290233847867">Just finished reading this book about 20 minutes ago. I have read Craig Clevenger's first novel, "The Contortionist's Handbook" as well and enjoyed them both. I think that "Dermaphoria" was even better than "Contortionist's..." though, which is a good thing because as a writer, you always want to be improving.<br/><br/>What I most enjoyed about this story were 1) the great sensitivity of the main character; 2) the brilliant & concise writing style and 3) Clevenger's mastery of metaphorical writing, which </span>
  <span id="freeText12079679290233847867" style="display:none">Just finished reading this book about 20 minutes ago. I have read Craig Clevenger's first novel, "The Contortionist's Handbook" as well and enjoyed them both. I think that "Dermaphoria" was even better than "Contortionist's..." though, which is a good thing because as a writer, you always want to be improving.<br /><br />What I most enjoyed about this story were 1) the great sensitivity of the main character; 2) the brilliant &amp; concise writing style and 3) Clevenger's mastery of metaphorical writing, which I admire greatly. He uses this style to its greatest effect &amp; better than any writer I have read. What makes his metaphors stand out is that they are completely unique which makes their impact that much greater.<br /><br />I found the story to be interesting as well, although a little bit confusing. But that is to be somewhat expected with stories that blur the line between reality and the imagination. By the book's end, I felt that I understood correctly what had occurred, but maybe not. And maybe that is part of the mastery of this story.<br /><br />I am greatly looking forward to the film version of this book, which is currently in production. It will be very interesting to see how many of the books ideas &amp; visuals-whether real or imagined- are played out onscreen.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="12079679290233847867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1073510390" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1073510390" />
    <a title="Melissa Monster " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10850028-melissa-monster"><img alt="Melissa Monster " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342458073p2/10850028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1073510390?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa Monster " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa Monster " href="/user/show/10850028-melissa-monster">Melissa Monster </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1073510390" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17956450810549935505">To describe Dermaphoria in the simplest, pop culture references, the story is like Walter White from Breaking Bad starring in a sequel of Memento.<br/>Now that that’s over…<br/>About a year ago, I decided my fiancé and I needed to be better versed on each other’s favorites. I wanted to see, eat, watch and most of all read what she loved. I made her a list of all the great experiences of my life, from the post-modern section of the DIA to Scarlett Thomas’ bewitching novel The End of Mr. Y. Then I relentles</span>
  <span id="freeText17956450810549935505" style="display:none">To describe Dermaphoria in the simplest, pop culture references, the story is like Walter White from Breaking Bad starring in a sequel of Memento.<br />Now that that’s over…<br />About a year ago, I decided my fiancé and I needed to be better versed on each other’s favorites. I wanted to see, eat, watch and most of all read what she loved. I made her a list of all the great experiences of my life, from the post-modern section of the DIA to Scarlett Thomas’ bewitching novel The End of Mr. Y. Then I relentlessly questioned her until I compiled a list of all her favorites. At the top of that list was The Contortionist’s Handbook, the debut novel of Craig Clevenger. The Handbook is the story of John Vincent, a master of forgery, whose migraine headaches and bad habits have locked him into a vicious circle of illegality. Clevenger’s ever-unique narrators grasp you from the first page and don’t let you go until they’re finished.<br />Dermaphoria opens with Eric Ashworth waking up in a hospital, his body is badly burned and his mind has no memory of how it got that way. He has the United States judicial system and the errand boys of a drug kingpin after him for answers. In order to spare himself a prison sentence or worse he has to find a way to remember his past. Ironically, it is the very drug that took Eric’s memory that helps him to bring it back.<br />“Write What You Know.”<br />Everyone’s heard that troubling phrase of non-wisdom. It makes the most inventive writers think of themselves with the most bland, paranoid thoughts. I don’t know enough! All I know is how to be a twenty something, unemployed Detroiter that likes girls and plays roller derby. Those are all the things that I know! The problems of “write what you know” stuck with me until my junior year of college when my playwriting teacher answered them saying, HELL NO! Write what you believe, learn what you know.<br />Running with this, one of the reasons Clevenger’s stories are so intriguing is the intricate level of research he completes for each. It gives him the ability to create the meticulously detailed thoughts of his characters. When you read a Craig Clevenger novel, you learn something about the skills of the narrator. In the Handbook, Vincent describes a practical passion for forgery, culminated from years using slight of hand. In Dermaphoria, Eric Ashworth is a chemist that invents a new drug that synthesizes the sensation of human touch and Clevenger displays knowledge of chemistry/pharmacology. The audience is pulled through the stories by the unique voice and knowledge of each narrator.<br />Revision of Re-Visions<br />The book has received lots of negative feedback for its readability. Or rather, its lack of. Dermaphoria literally puts you in the mind of an amnesiac drug addict. With each dose of Derma, he remembers pairs of invisible hands and with his withdrawal comes an avalanche of tactile reality and paranoia. When Eric questions what is real, you realize that you don’t know for yourself what it real. You experience his hallucinations, his paranoia, his flashbacks. Clevenger is sitting next to you the whole time, experiencing it all with you. The only difference is that he’s been here before. He’s been living in this world for years. In an interview for chuckpalaniuk.net, Clevenger describes his writing and revision for the novel. “I didn't write a complete draft and then re-write a series of drafts one after the next. Every chapter existed as a separate document up until a couple of weeks before I strung them all together for the press galley (Cite!.)” Every structure in the book has been designed to throw the audience into chaos from words to paragraphs to chapters. The key is to find the beauty in the chaos. (I am a great lover of chaos.)<br />When asked about his work for his third novel Clevenger references a short story available on his website titled, The Fade. He explains that his next novel will be an expansion of this story. <br /><br /><br />********<br /><br /><br />My book club chose this as our November novel but there were no discussion questions available! Came up with these to guide discussion. Thought they might be helpful for other book clubs!<br /><br /><br />Possible Discussion Questions-<br /><br />1. Eric wakes up with no memory of his life. As the story progresses, the audience learns only what Eric’s mind chooses to remember. How does this challenge your reading? As you read, how are your own opinions colored by this fact?<br /><br />2a. Clevenger uses many different motifs to propel his story. Which motifs did you find throughout the book? Which of these do you think works best in the story.<br /><br />2b. Discuss the significance of fire and it’s many occurrences throughout the novel.<br /><br />3. Throughout the narrative the name Desiree is associated with many different situations. Discuss the many different references. Who or what do you believe is the true Desiree.<br /><br />4. Discuss Eric’s understanding of God. Is Eric attempting to “play God” in his creation of Derma or is he finding a way to become closer to God by altering he mental states of its participants.<br /><br />5. In an interview with Chuck Palanuk.net Clevenger talks about the use of bugs in the novel, “The early versions of the story had Eric recalling part of his youth spent with Pentecostal snake handlers, so there was as much narrative devoted to reptile imagery as there was to bugs, but I cut those sections out. They weren't important to the story, so they had to go.”<br />Discuss the many ways bugs are used in the story, why are these sections so important to the narrative?<br />6. “I didn't write a complete draft and then re-write a series of drafts one after the next. Every chapter existed as a separate document up until a couple of weeks before I strung them all together for the press galley.”<br /><br />How does this writing style complement the narrative? How does it harm the narrative? If you re-ordered the chapters in the novel, would the overall story still make sense?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17956450810549935505" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Logan Person" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/92251735-logan-person"><img alt="Logan Person" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2676926066?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Logan Person" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Logan Person" href="/user/show/92251735-logan-person">Logan Person</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2676926066" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16482698884830095215">Initially, I didn't lile this one as much as tchb, but after reading it a second time I fou nd it to be a super fun story! Breaking bad meets fear and loathing in LV + a little bit of the sound and the fury.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_5238442" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5238442" />
    <a title="Jason Pettus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus"><img alt="Jason Pettus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548377232p2/147289.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5238442?book_show_action=true">Aug 28, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Pettus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Pettus" href="/user/show/147289-jason-pettus">Jason Pettus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5238442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9610164737810106805">(The much longer full review can be found at the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com:].)<br/><br/>Okay, I confess: that of all the different types of underground artists out there, I have a particular affinity for the weird quiet ones on the edge of every scene, who frequently engage in cutting-edge experiments just for the sake of engaging in them. For example, when I was involved with the performance-poetry community of the 1990s, I tended to spend a lot of time with the peopl</span>
  <span id="freeText9610164737810106805" style="display:none">(The much longer full review can be found at the Chicago Center for Literature and Photography [cclapcenter.com:].)<br /><br />Okay, I confess: that of all the different types of underground artists out there, I have a particular affinity for the weird quiet ones on the edge of every scene, who frequently engage in cutting-edge experiments just for the sake of engaging in them. For example, when I was involved with the performance-poetry community of the 1990s, I tended to spend a lot of time with the people who would drag weird abstract "musical instruments" on stage with them; who would slip the cadences of formal poetry into their work and then not tell anyone, just to see if anyone would notice anyway. And even better, of course, when that artist is quirkily attractive, always jittery in a way that could be innocent or guilty (depending on what substances they've ingested earlier in the evening, I suppose), a literal walking example of what many of us think when we hear the phrase "Life In The Big City."<br /><br />I just got done with a new book by such an artist, in fact, the profoundly dense and sometimes outright perplexing <i>dermaphoria</i> by San Franciscan Craig Clevenger, whose first novel <i>The Contortionist's Handbook</i> became an accidental sleeper hit in 2003. It is a book along the same vein as <i>Memento</i>, <i>Me and Kev</i>, <i>The Prestige</i> and others, where a dark tale is told in a puzzling manner, slowly releasing bits and pieces of information until the entire story is finally revealed by the end; and this is a type of book I love, to be honest, for the same reason I love hyperfiction and the like, although acknowledge as well that it's not everyone's cup of tea. And similarly, Clevenger wields a personal writing style here that is deliberately over-the-top self-conscious, deliberately showy and obtuse; and again, you're either going to like it like I did, or detest it and have it get in the way of you enjoying the novel whatsoever. That's the whole point of being wildly experimental, after all; to throw off <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1812457.The_Shack" title="The Shack by William P. Young" rel="nofollow noopener">the shack</a>les of popularity, to know in advance that the project will appeal to only a slim amount of people, but that they'll be profoundly moved by it instead of a zillion people shrugging their shoulders and going, "Meh."<br /><br />Much like <i>Memento</i> (and several other high-profile stories that have come out this year)...</span>
  <a data-text-id="9610164737810106805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17532925051314997861" style="display:none">Sometimes I read a book and it's the story that stands out, or even the characters, but in Dermaphoria it is the author who stands out and Clevenger is right there on every page. For me, this book isn't just about the character, or about what is or is not real, it was about how we all formulate our ideas of reality and then act accordingly and how much power the people around us have in influencing that idea of reality.<br />This is a gritty and personally enriching work that doesn't pull any punches.</span>

<div id="review_527254851" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/527254851" />
    <a title="Dina Hekal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4775337-dina-hekal"><img alt="Dina Hekal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1559160665p2/4775337.jpg" /></a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789773515376" href="/book/show/9304885">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4775337-dina-hekal?shelf=i-have">i-have</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4775337-dina-hekal?shelf=arabic">arabic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4775337-dina-hekal?shelf=arabic-read">arabic-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4775337-dina-hekal?shelf=paper">paper</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer527254851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5326009743069658308">Ok, where do i start? Well, this story feels upside down. The writing is crazy like the main character. It feels like a movie more than a novel, the scenes are described vividly. The craziness in the novel comes from Eric the main character, who starts the story with memory loss, and in trial for creating drugs. He seemed to be a very good chemist for his drugs left the city needing more. His memory loss came from being brain dead for eight minutes after overdosing which rendered him in the stat</span>
  <span id="freeText5326009743069658308" style="display:none">Ok, where do i start? Well, this story feels upside down. The writing is crazy like the main character. It feels like a movie more than a novel, the scenes are described vividly. The craziness in the novel comes from Eric the main character, who starts the story with memory loss, and in trial for creating drugs. He seemed to be a very good chemist for his drugs left the city needing more. His memory loss came from being brain dead for eight minutes after overdosing which rendered him in the state we met him. Unable to tell what's real and what's not. The story is a mix between paranoid hallucinations, some real events and false/correct flashbacks. Eric's life is a mess between the police led by detective Anslinger who wants to put him in jail, his lawyer who knew more about him than he did, the gang members wanting their money and drugs, the pains of his condition, and his search for his lost love Desiree. The love story between Eric and her was amazing. The way he spoke of her, describing and telling her how much he loved her was so sweet. The way he called for her was so sweet that I heard it in my mind every time he said her name "Desiree."<br />Most of the story I was unable to know what is happening :D which was great. This added a new sense of mystery and made me think about every line written. I did get lost a lot though. And some plot twists felt like a blow to the head, my head actually hurt :D The novel ended with more question than its start for me and made me want to read it again. The missing star for the unanswered question that will haunt me for the rest of life. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="5326009743069658308" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review527254851">

<div id="review_356030133" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/356030133" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9612994-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1337354005p2/9612994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/356030133?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/9612994-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer356030133" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9824462838149321184">Good book overall. Hard to follow the fractured "psychedelic" narrative structure at times, though the author makes a heroic attempt to experiment with the very structure of how a story can be told. Clevenger skillfully captures the splintered paranoia of the psychedelic experience. The plot could be described as one long "bad trip" as seen through the eyes of the book's antihero/protagonist Eric; a nightmarish dreamscape rife with legions of insectoid spies working for "them", nefarious silent </span>
  <span id="freeText9824462838149321184" style="display:none">Good book overall. Hard to follow the fractured "psychedelic" narrative structure at times, though the author makes a heroic attempt to experiment with the very structure of how a story can be told. Clevenger skillfully captures the splintered paranoia of the psychedelic experience. The plot could be described as one long "bad trip" as seen through the eyes of the book's antihero/protagonist Eric; a nightmarish dreamscape rife with legions of insectoid spies working for "them", nefarious silent black helicopters, the death of the ego and the unique horror that comes from the blurring of reality, fantasy and nightmare. Peppered between the fantastical elements of psychedelia is the story of an ingenious young chemist (Eric) who has been hired by professional criminals as the brains behind an illicit recreational pharmaceutical manufacturing operation (LSD, MDMA, Methamphetamine and a new drug called "Touch" from which the title of the book is derived). Think "Breaking Bad" told from a mind-bending kaleidoscopic perspective. There is also a twisted love story sub-plot. Tough to read at times, but, overall, a good second effort by Clevenger. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9824462838149321184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer3644086220097544717">Craig Clevenger is a brilliant writer, and I'm quite excited to read a great follow-up to "The Contortionist's Handbook". "Dermaphoria" just wasn't that "great follow-up"; a fellow reviewer remarked that this book was a classic example of "the sophomore slump", and I'd have to agree.<br/><br/>"Dermaphoria" doesn't break any new ground; it's almost a carbon copy of "Contortionist's..". The protagonist in this one is an intelligent chemist who creates a Wonderdrug called "Derma" and gets high on his own sup</span>
  <span id="freeText3644086220097544717" style="display:none">Craig Clevenger is a brilliant writer, and I'm quite excited to read a great follow-up to "The Contortionist's Handbook". "Dermaphoria" just wasn't that "great follow-up"; a fellow reviewer remarked that this book was a classic example of "the sophomore slump", and I'd have to agree.<br /><br />"Dermaphoria" doesn't break any new ground; it's almost a carbon copy of "Contortionist's..". The protagonist in this one is an intelligent chemist who creates a Wonderdrug called "Derma" and gets high on his own supply. Clevenger's gimmick for this book is to tell the story in "flashback", with the flashbacks told as the protagonist's drug-induced flashbacks. This gimmick is so over-used that after about 100 pages I found my self getting so lost in the drug-induced imagery that I began not caring what was happening or why. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3644086220097544717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_673533993" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/673533993" />
    <a title="Trevor Pearson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13458191-trevor-pearson"><img alt="Trevor Pearson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520916427p2/13458191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/673533993?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trevor Pearson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trevor Pearson" href="/user/show/13458191-trevor-pearson">Trevor Pearson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13458191-trevor-pearson?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer673533993" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10626837346006135966"><blockquote>
  <i>“If I'm still conscious to face the consequences of my actions, then at the very least I will know that my actions were real, they indeed had consequences, though my lone life will amount to less than a single click of static in the symphony of the big bang. If my actions were real, then so were my memories, and if those were real, the things I've done have allowed me to see God and I0m not afraid of following my life down that eight-second black rabbit hole.”  </i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>A new street drug that has app</span>
  <span id="freeText10626837346006135966" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>“If I'm still conscious to face the consequences of my actions, then at the very least I will know that my actions were real, they indeed had consequences, though my lone life will amount to less than a single click of static in the symphony of the big bang. If my actions were real, then so were my memories, and if those were real, the things I've done have allowed me to see God and I0m not afraid of following my life down that eight-second black rabbit hole.”  </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />A new street drug that has apparently opened a window to God, some addicts would simply call that good old fashioned opiods at work, but an unassuming experimental chemist named Eric Ashworth had a plan to do something nobody else had ever thought possible, to manufacture a chemical cure for all bad feelings essentially for the price of a tank of gas. Drugs play a big role in this story but Dermaphoria is more than that, it is a story of lost time where periods as long as years and short as minutes leave the main character confused as he awakens in jail being asked questions he had no answers for. Most of the accused play this cat and mouse game with police interrogators but not with their own defence attorney, unfortunately for Eric this was no game being played. All Eric had to offer his lawyer was a woman’s name: Desiree. Everything else is lost, but if he knew what was good for him, Eric would do what he could to put the puzzle back together even if it meant going back to where his memory began to fade, he just needed a remedy. Eric awakens in a constant state of analysis, like a newborn baby, his world is taking shape before his very own eyes. His body is badly burned, wrapped in bandages far away from the folk hero/ mystical creature lore he earned while working his magic in the Californian desert. He is advised by his lawyer to not discuss the case with anyone, which in this case is an easy proposition, but as time goes on his brain replenishes aided by ye old hair of the dog, and with that, people from his sketchy past become more fleshed out and memories slowly come back to him. Eric is released on bail and sets up shop in a local roach motel where other criminals and past acquaintances take up a temporary residence. His paranoia gives him a heightened awareness of his new surroundings and he learns quickly that he’s being followed and bugged by the LAPD. Old friends learn about his five day release and begin pushing and pulling him in different directions all with their own motivations as the main framework for action. Bail in this case is not a means for freedom, that will take time, and time is running out. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“When you could discern a real threat from everything else, it was called caution. When you couldn't, it was called paranoia. Like someone who heard every noise at the same volume, the sounds were always there, you became crazy when you heard them all at once. <br />You cannot separate paranoia from knowledge. The more you know, the more possibilities you see. The more possibilities you see, the more possibilities someone else sees. The more "someones" there are, the more "they" there are. It's a matter of simple math before you realize that They might not like you.”  </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />In what seems like a past life Eric was a blue chip, can’t miss prospect in the drug manufacturing game. His reputation grew with each batch created and distributed to financial districts, local night clubs, office buildings and schools. The name of the drug may have changed on the daily but the sole man behind the new trend in the industry would remain the same, he would become a hot commodity, and with deep pocketed individuals looking for the next big thing, they wouldn’t be denied. The primary funding for this enterprising Fortune 500 run drug business came from a man named Hoyle. Hoyle may have been the chief financier looking more for a profit and building his reserves off the backs of the hopeless wanderers living in Los Angeles, but he was desperately looking for someone to manufacture a drug that made people desire, want, need, cant live without; he wanted people to crave MORE. Hoyle would meet Erik thanks to the man responsible for handling the recruitment and selection, as well as the control of the day to day operations of the lab, this man was known as Manhattan White (which is also a pretty good identifier for a drug if you ask me). What’s even more frightening than white collars in the dangerous drug game were malevolent henchmen with the moniker Toe Tag, causing even more disturbance to the experimental process was that the intellectually stunted Toe Tag happened to be Mr. White's son, and what a devoted son he was and would prove to be as the story developed. Everything started out fine, Eric had a temporary contract that granted him a large sum of money for service and an end date so he could get out before it got too hot. The headquarters where all the magic happened was called Oz and was appropriately located in a Californian desert seemingly cut off from the rest of the world. What Eric quickly realizes is that it’s amateur hour in Oz, and when dealing with the combustible material they have laid out everyone had to be on point. There would be injuries and deaths that would go unreported, the main priority was to ensure his own safety which was not an inevitability in a place like this with the slack jaws he shares company with. Work slowly begins getting to Eric, the pressure of meeting the market demand and from his unseemly boss is starting to negatively impact his relationship with Desiree. Eric is also a methodical individual but has trouble dealing with people. He needs to have people hate him in order to keep them safe which would prove to be important when the rubber met the road. <br /><br /> <blockquote>
  <i>“My food arrived. I smelled methyl chloride residue, used to decaffeinate the coffee. The diner probably held fifty or sixty pounds in their storeroom, enough trace methyl to bind to a hundred different hosts and reinvent the chemical wheel. Nudge a molecule, an atom. The difference between amphetamine and methamphetamine is both miniscule and gargantuan, and that guillotined elk head knew it, staring at me from its cedar trophy mount trying to look stupid.” </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />The narrative and pace best complemented the subject matter and state of mind of the main character. With all of the paranoia, hallucinations, and bugs which may or may not be real, it reminded me of a little known film called A Scanner Darkly and The Salton Sea with the messing around with time sprinkled with a David Lynch as there is a consistent mention of the humming of electricity which is used as a symbol and a gateway for an approaching evil. Overall Dermaphoria by Craig Clevenger was an intriguing read that kept you on your toes and head slightly askew. The author challenges the reader with the idea of time right from the beginning as we are introduced to an unreliable narrator whose perceptions are a point of discovery and memory which is all but lost waiting to be found in a very fractured state. Overall Dermaphoria was a good read but I enjoyed The Contortionist's Handbook more because of its faster pace and it’s ability to make me care, but still a good change of pace if you need one. <br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>“The difference between the man on parole and the man on death row is sometimes two inches of locked bathroom door or a single moment’s hesitation. The difference between those men and a chimpanzee is two percent of their genes and the difference between a man's healthy tissue and his tumor is even less. Every man and every insect are made from the same six molecules of DNA, the same five atoms. One of those atoms makes the difference between speed and cold medicine, between paint thinner and TNT. Every identical act is distinguished by its intent and every intent is judged by its action. The difference between consent and rape can be a single drink or a single word. Everything in the universe is everything else. A man is a killer is a saint is a monkey is a cockroach is a goldfish is a whale, and the Devil is just the angel who asked for More.”</i>
</blockquote> <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10626837346006135966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14568202043289233253" style="display:none">I love this book, it is from a great new author. The main character is under the influence of a new experimental drug that he has been developing. From what I can tell it is a mash up of meth and acid, so his thoughts are pretty intense and most of the story through passages like this one:<br /><br />"After my heart has bloomed to the size of the universe and all the love from the big bang to the last whisper has been cycloning through my chest for what feels like days on end, the world is one giant prison when the storm dies, at last. The galaxies shrink back to the lump of muscle behind my ribs, the sniper's target just to the left of my spine. The sleepless night and following day weigh down like a leaden gray forever. It feels like dying.<br />I thought I missed you, Desiree. I had no idea how much."<br /><br />It's a really good story and it is told in a very unique way. His first novel "The Contortionist's Handbook" is also very good. Check him out if you get the chance. If you don't have the time to work him in to your busy reading schedules, he has some great short stories up on his website as well, though I feel like the novels best represent him.<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.craigclevenger.com">www.craigclevenger.com</a><br /><br />enjoy!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer72114801595706527">Craig Clevenger is a brilliant writer, and I'm quite excited to read a great follow-up to "The Contortionist's Handbook". "Dermaphoria" just wasn't that "great follow-up"; a fellow reviewer remarked that this book was a classic example of "the sophomore slump", and I'd have to agree.<br/><br/>"Dermaphoria" doesn't break any new ground; it's almost a carbon copy of "Contortionist's..". The protagonist in this one is an intelligent chemist who creates a Wonderdrug called "Derma" and gets high on his own sup</span>
  <span id="freeText72114801595706527" style="display:none">Craig Clevenger is a brilliant writer, and I'm quite excited to read a great follow-up to "The Contortionist's Handbook". "Dermaphoria" just wasn't that "great follow-up"; a fellow reviewer remarked that this book was a classic example of "the sophomore slump", and I'd have to agree.<br /><br />"Dermaphoria" doesn't break any new ground; it's almost a carbon copy of "Contortionist's..". The protagonist in this one is an intelligent chemist who creates a Wonderdrug called "Derma" and gets high on his own supply. Clevenger's gimmick for this book is to tell the story in "flashback", with the flashbacks told as the protagonist's drug-induced flashbacks. This gimmick is so over-used that after about 100 pages I found my self getting so lost in the drug-induced imagery that I began not caring what was happening or why. </span>
  <a data-text-id="72114801595706527" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer1705198578317287657">Quite a fragmented narrative, which is intentional due to the main characters memory loss, but still makes for a difficuly read at times.<br/><br/>The story is a little familiar, maybe even cliched, the guy who can't remember what he's done, and finds out it's worse than he thinks. Some good bits but a lot of jarring moments. Every time it gets going (and it gets in a cool groove quite often) it suddenly cuts back to some nonsensical 'drugs make you think weird thoughts' schtick.<br/><br/>The paranoia and bits wher</span>
  <span id="freeText1705198578317287657" style="display:none">Quite a fragmented narrative, which is intentional due to the main characters memory loss, but still makes for a difficuly read at times.<br /><br />The story is a little familiar, maybe even cliched, the guy who can't remember what he's done, and finds out it's worse than he thinks. Some good bits but a lot of jarring moments. Every time it gets going (and it gets in a cool groove quite often) it suddenly cuts back to some nonsensical 'drugs make you think weird thoughts' schtick.<br /><br />The paranoia and bits where he's convinced there's people listening through insects in his room was a bit derivative (Dick/Burroughs kinda thing)and took up way too much of the book, although would have been a short story without it, which I guess is the problem I had with it. Needed more happening and less talking to himself.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12433192-alex?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer405152251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12007903421123426350">I started reading this book a couple years ago, got 80 some pages, and stopped. It wasn't sucking me in, and I didn't think I liked it very much. I picked it up again recently, reread those 80 pages and then another 50, and stopped again. Finally I picked it back up, determined to finish it, and plowed through the rest of it in no time flat.<br/><br/>This book is a bit of a tough read, though I didn't really understand that at first. I mistook mystery for a writing style that I simply wasn't enjoying, but</span>
  <span id="freeText12007903421123426350" style="display:none">I started reading this book a couple years ago, got 80 some pages, and stopped. It wasn't sucking me in, and I didn't think I liked it very much. I picked it up again recently, reread those 80 pages and then another 50, and stopped again. Finally I picked it back up, determined to finish it, and plowed through the rest of it in no time flat.<br /><br />This book is a bit of a tough read, though I didn't really understand that at first. I mistook mystery for a writing style that I simply wasn't enjoying, but as I pressed on and started putting the pieces together it finally clicked.<br /><br />It's a confusing read at first, but by the end I realized how wonderfully written it was. The story wouldn't have been so fascinating if it had been told in a straightforward manner. By putting us through the same confused nature of discovery as the protagonist, it rewards the reader far more.</span>

<div id="review_1461306736" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1461306736" />
    <a title="Endless Warner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11083951-endless-warner"><img alt="Endless Warner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469644560p2/11083951.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1461306736?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Endless Warner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Endless Warner" href="/user/show/11083951-endless-warner">Endless Warner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11083951-endless-warner?shelf=strangers-in-stranglands">strangers-in-stranglands</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1461306736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1875958151692750717">I read this book not long after a car accident that gave me amnesia and left me with a month of fragmented memories. <br/>Clevenger's writing style moves with raw class, leaving behind fifty cent words where nickel and dime words work perfectly. The drug chemist culture and search for the woman he's trying to remember sucked me in as I tried to remember the EMT that took my hand when walking to the ambulance. <br/>I admit, I don't remember this book with a clear memory, but one that is within the same fuz</span>
  <span id="freeText1875958151692750717" style="display:none">I read this book not long after a car accident that gave me amnesia and left me with a month of fragmented memories. <br />Clevenger's writing style moves with raw class, leaving behind fifty cent words where nickel and dime words work perfectly. The drug chemist culture and search for the woman he's trying to remember sucked me in as I tried to remember the EMT that took my hand when walking to the ambulance. <br />I admit, I don't remember this book with a clear memory, but one that is within the same fuzzy, slightly drugged out frame of mind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1875958151692750717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_7527492" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7527492" />
    <a title="Kurt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/292778-kurt"><img alt="Kurt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1264385256p2/292778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7527492?book_show_action=true">Oct 10, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kurt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kurt" href="/user/show/292778-kurt">Kurt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone and everyone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7527492" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer612327569466621595">another great one from Clevenger: the chain, the organization, from the point of view of another link (re: Contortionist's Handbook). Rather, another protagonist getting by with intelligence and drugs, wrapped up in the chain, looking for the patterns (equations in CH, chemical compounds in this one) that will explain things. Same great writing, but more lyrical this time. According to his website, a third story is in process: whatever it is, I'll read it.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_21309030" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21309030" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1053637-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21309030?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/1053637-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21309030" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11937669192300583509">Clevenger does a standout job of conveying the main character's paranoia and while there are some far out passages, I didn't get lost too much in his drug induced frenzied paragraphs.<br/><br/>I was paranoid myself reading the book with the various types of insects eating away at my thoughts.<br/><br/>I can't really pinpoint why I liked this book so much, but I did and recommend it if you liked Fight Club or any of Chuck Palahnuik’s work.<br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_305410673" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/305410673" />
    <a title="J." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1271522-j"><img alt="J." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459919037p2/1271522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/305410673?book_show_action=true">Apr 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J." class="user" itemprop="url" name="J." href="/user/show/1271522-j">J.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer305410673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5920324940536485792">Read it because Chuck Palahniuk said it was the best book he'd read in the last few years. He wasn't wrong. The prose is powerful and twisting, the best kind of concrete poetry. Eric is one of the most interesting unreliable narrators I've met in a while. Don't listen to most of the low-ratings reviews: the fragmentation of the narrative isn't in the way of the story--the fragmentation IS the story. Also boasts one of the best last chapters I've ever read. HIGHLY recommended.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_818933874" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/818933874" />
    <a title="Philipp" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8325737-philipp"><img alt="Philipp" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1392618768p2/8325737.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/818933874?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Philipp" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Philipp" href="/user/show/8325737-philipp">Philipp</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8325737-philipp?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8325737-philipp?shelf=drugs">drugs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer818933874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6374315954606865398">Do you remember that over-the-top drug dude from A Scanner Darkly?<br/><br/>This book is what it would have been like if that dude became a Heisenberg-like genius drug cook who lost his memory and has to slowly piece it all back together. Sometimes hard to read since the narrator is beyond "unreliable" and the language becomes very poetic, but it's still entertaining. Yet I probably will forget this book in 2 months.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3roX0bpaHHdxXd5QBRQN8ch5hvNEMXTHV1y6bPrbx/MCnJLTObQmkokFE4LDXdHPtIcMNlf1P0w3azDcy+iTxQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4M69XNhedJ+Q071I1ej8HrmZiXI1qBEGeFMmfJdDfto86DheW7BOemiLcJoToSAgxWcDtyZsWo0YZKzMpnAq7A==')})
  }
//]]>

Overrun with paranoia, teenage angst, and religious zeal in the massacre\'s wake, this sleepy suburban neighborhood declares its saints, brands its demons, and moves on. But for a handful of people still reeling from that horrific day, life remains permanently derailed. Four dramatically different characters tell their stories: Cheryl, who calmly narrates her own death; Jason, the boy no one knew was her husband, still marooned ten years later by his loss; Heather, the woman trying to love the shattered Jason; and Jason\'s father, Reg, whose rigid religiosity has separated him from nearly everyone he loves.
